Between 2013 and 2018, nearly 700,000 migrants reached Italy by boat, many of them from Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-134"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-134">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-135">[132]</a></sup> In May 2018 Libya's rival leaders agreed to hold parliamentary and presidential elections following a meeting in Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-136"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-136">[133]</a></sup> In April 2019, [Khalifa Haftar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khalifa_Haftar "Khalifa Haftar") launched [Operation Flood of Dignity](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Libya_campaign "Western Libya campaign"), in an offensive by the [Libyan National Army](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_National_Army "Libyan National Army") aimed to seize Western territories from the [Government of National Accord](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_National_Accord "Government of National Accord") (GNA).<sup id="cite_ref-137"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-137">[134]</a></sup> In June 2019, forces allied to Libya's UN-recognized Government of National Accord successfully captured Gharyan, a strategic town where military commander Khalifa Haftar and his fighters were based. According to a spokesman for GNA forces, Mustafa al-Mejii, dozens of LNA fighters under Haftar were killed, while at least 18 were taken prisoner.<sup id="cite_ref-138"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-138">[135]</a></sup>

In March 2020, UN-backed government of [Fayez Al-Sarraj](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fayez_al-Sarraj "Fayez al-Sarraj") commenced Operation Peace Storm. The government initiated the bid in response to the state of assaults carried by [Haftar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khalifa_Haftar "Khalifa Haftar")'s [LNA](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_National_Army "Libyan National Army"). "We are a legitimate, civilian government that respects its obligations to the international community, but is committed primarily to its people and has an obligation to protect its citizens," Sarraj said in line with his decision.<sup id="cite_ref-139"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-139">[136]</a></sup> On 28 August 2020, the _[BBC](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/BBC "BBC") Africa Eye_ and _BBC Arabic Documentaries_ revealed that a drone operated by the [United Arab Emirates](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates "United Arab Emirates") (UAE) killed 26 young cadets at a military academy in [Tripoli](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tripoli,_Libya "Tripoli, Libya"), on 4 January. Most of the cadets were teenagers and none of them were armed. The Chinese-made drone [Wing Loong II](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/CAIG_Wing_Loong_II "CAIG Wing Loong II") fired Blue Arrow 7 missile, which was operated from UAE-run Al-Khadim Libyan air base. In February, these drones stationed in Libya were moved to an air base near [Siwa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siwa_Oasis "Siwa Oasis") in the western Egyptian desert.<sup id="cite_ref-140"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-140">[137]</a></sup> _[The Guardian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Guardian "The Guardian")_ probed and discovered the blatant violation of [UN](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations "United Nations") arms embargo by the [UAE](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates "United Arab Emirates") and [Turkey](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkey "Turkey") on 7 October 2020. As per the reporting, both the nations sent large-scale military cargo planes to Libya in support of their respective parties.<sup id="cite_ref-141"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-141">[138]</a></sup> On 23 October 2020, [a permanent ceasefire](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_peace_process "Libyan peace process") was signed to end the war.<sup id="cite_ref-142"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-142">[139]</a></sup>

### Post-civil war years [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: Post-civil war years")

Learn more

This section **needs expansion**. You can help by [adding to it](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=). _(December 2021)_

In December 2021, the [country's first presidential election](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/2022_Libyan_presidential_election "2022 Libyan presidential election") was scheduled, but was delayed to June 2022<sup id="cite_ref-143"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-143">[140]</a></sup> and later postponed further.

[Fathi Bashagha](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fathi_Bashagha "Fathi Bashagha") was appointed prime minister by the parliament in February 2022 to lead a transitional administration, but standing prime minister [Abdul Hamid Dbeibeh](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdul_Hamid_Dbeibeh "Abdul Hamid Dbeibeh") refused to hand over power as of April 2022. In protest against the Dbeibah government, tribal leaders from the desert town of [Ubari](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ubari "Ubari") shut down the [El Sharara oil field](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/El_Sharara_oil_field "El Sharara oil field"), Libya's largest oil field, on 18 April 2022. The shut down threatened to cause oil shortages domestically in Libya, and preclude the state-run National Oil Corp. from exploiting the high oil prices on the international market resulting from the [Russian invasion of Ukraine](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "Russian invasion of Ukraine").<sup id="cite_ref-144"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-144">[141]</a></sup> On 2 July, the [House of Representatives](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Representatives_(Libya) "House of Representatives (Libya)") was burned down by protesters.<sup id="cite_ref-145"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-145">[142]</a></sup>

On September 10, 2023, catastrophic floods due to [dam failures](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derna_dam_collapses "Derna dam collapses") generated by [Storm Daniel](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Storm_Daniel "Storm Daniel") devastated the port city of [Derna](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derna,_Libya "Derna, Libya"), killing more than 4,000 and leaving over 10,000 missing. The floods were the worst natural disaster in Libya's modern history.<sup id="cite_ref-October_3_update_146-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-October_3_update-146">[143]</a></sup>

## Geography [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: Geography")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Un-libya.png)

A map of Libya

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_LBY_present.svg)

Libya map of Köppen climate classification

Libya extends over 1,759,540 square kilometres (679,362 sq mi), making it the [16th-largest nation in the world by size](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area "List of countries and dependencies by area"). Libya is bounded to the north by the [Mediterranean Sea](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea "Mediterranean Sea"), the west by [Tunisia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tunisia "Tunisia") and [Algeria](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algeria "Algeria"), the southwest by [Niger](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Niger "Niger"), the south by [Chad](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chad "Chad"), the southeast by [Sudan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sudan "Sudan"), and the east by [Egypt](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egypt "Egypt"). Libya lies between latitudes [19°](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/19th_parallel_north "19th parallel north") and [34°N](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/34th_parallel_north "34th parallel north"), and longitudes [9°](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/9th_meridian_east "9th meridian east") and [26°E](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/26th_meridian_east "26th meridian east").

At 1,770 kilometres (1,100 mi), Libya's coastline is the longest of any African country bordering the Mediterranean.<sup id="cite_ref-147"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-147">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-148">[145]</a></sup> The portion of the Mediterranean Sea north of Libya is often called the [Libyan Sea](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_Sea "Libyan Sea"). The climate is mostly extremely dry and desertlike in nature. However, the northern regions enjoy a milder [Mediterranean climate](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mediterranean_climate "Mediterranean climate").<sup id="cite_ref-149"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-149">[146]</a></sup>

Six ecoregions lie within Libya's borders: [Saharan halophytics](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saharan_halophytics "Saharan halophytics"), [Mediterranean dry woodlands and steppe](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mediterranean_dry_woodlands_and_steppe "Mediterranean dry woodlands and steppe"), [Mediterranean woodlands and forests](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mediterranean_woodlands_and_forests "Mediterranean woodlands and forests"), [North Saharan steppe and woodlands](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Saharan_steppe_and_woodlands "North Saharan steppe and woodlands"), [Tibesti-Jebel Uweinat montane xeric woodlands](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tibesti-Jebel_Uweinat_montane_xeric_woodlands "Tibesti-Jebel Uweinat montane xeric woodlands"), and [West Saharan montane xeric woodlands](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Saharan_montane_xeric_woodlands "West Saharan montane xeric woodlands").<sup id="cite_ref-DinersteinOlson2017_150-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-DinersteinOlson2017-150">[147]</a></sup>

Natural hazards come in the form of hot, dry, dust-laden [sirocco](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sirocco "Sirocco") (known in Libya as the _gibli_). This is a southern wind blowing from one to four days in spring and autumn. There are also [dust storms](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dust_storm "Dust storm") and [sandstorms](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dust_storm "Dust storm"). [Oases](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oasis "Oasis") can also be found scattered throughout Libya, the most important of which are [Ghadames](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghadames "Ghadames") and [Kufra](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kufra "Kufra").<sup id="cite_ref-WorldCulturalHeritage_151-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-WorldCulturalHeritage-151">[148]</a></sup> Libya is one of the sunniest and driest countries in the world due to prevailing presence of desert environment.<sup>[<i><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>

Libya was a pioneer state in North Africa in species protection, with the creation in 1975 of the El Kouf protected area. The fall of Muammar Gaddafi's regime favoured intense [poaching](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poaching "Poaching"): "Before the fall of Gaddafi even hunting rifles were forbidden. But since 2011, poaching has been carried out with weapons of war and sophisticated vehicles in which one can find up to 200 gazelle heads killed by militiamen who hunt to pass the time. We are also witnessing the emergence of hunters with no connection to the tribes that traditionally practice hunting. They shoot everything they find, even during the breeding season. More than 500,000 birds are killed in this way each year, when protected areas have been seized by tribal chiefs who have appropriated them. The animals that used to live there have all disappeared, hunted when they are edible or released when they are not," explains zoologist Khaled Ettaieb.<sup id="cite_ref-152"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-152">[149]</a></sup>

### Libyan Desert [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: Libyan Desert")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Libyan_Desert_-_2006.jpg)

Libya is a predominantly desert country. Over 95% of the land area is covered in desert.<sup id="cite_ref-153"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-153">[150]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Water_Stress,_Top_Countries_(2020).svg)

Libya is the fourth-most water-stressed country in the world.

The [Libyan Desert](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_Desert "Libyan Desert"), which covers most of Libya, is one of the most arid and sun-baked places on earth.<sup id="cite_ref-SalakLibya_63-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-SalakLibya-63">[60]</a></sup> In places, decades may pass without seeing any rainfall at all, and even in the [highlands](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highland_(geography) "Highland (geography)") rainfall seldom happens, once every 5–10 years. At [Uweinat](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jebel_Uweinat "Jebel Uweinat"), as of 2006 the last recorded rainfall was in September 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-Libdesert_154-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Libdesert-154">[151]</a></sup>

Likewise, the temperature in the Libyan Desert can be extreme; on 13 September 1922, the town of ['Aziziya](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/%27Aziziya "'Aziziya"), which is located southwest of [Tripoli](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tripoli,_Libya "Tripoli, Libya"), recorded an air temperature of 58 °C (136.4 °F), considered to be a world record.<sup id="cite_ref-155"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-155">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Death_Valley_156-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Death_Valley-156">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-El_Azizia_157-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-El_Azizia-157">[154]</a></sup> In September 2012, however, the world record figure of 58 °C was determined to be invalid by the [World Meteorological Organization](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization "World Meteorological Organization").<sup id="cite_ref-Death_Valley_156-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Death_Valley-156">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-El_Azizia_157-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-El_Azizia-157">[154]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-158">[155]</a></sup>

There are a few scattered uninhabited small oases, usually linked to the major depressions, where water can be found by digging to a few feet in depth. In the west there is a widely dispersed group of oases in unconnected shallow depressions, the Kufra group, consisting of Tazerbo, Rebianae and [Kufra](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kufra "Kufra").<sup id="cite_ref-Libdesert_154-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Libdesert-154">[151]</a></sup> Aside from the scarps, the general flatness is only interrupted by a series of [plateaus](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plateau "Plateau") and massifs near the centre of the Libyan Desert, around the convergence of the Egyptian-Sudanese-Libyan borders.

Slightly further to the south are the [massifs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Massif "Massif") of Arkenu, Uweinat, and Kissu. These [granite](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Granite "Granite") mountains are ancient, having formed long before the sandstones surrounding them. Arkenu and Western Uweinat are ring complexes very similar to those in the [Aïr Mountains](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/A%C3%AFr_Mountains "Aïr Mountains"). Eastern Uweinat (the highest point in the Libyan Desert) is a raised sandstone plateau adjacent to the granite part further west.<sup id="cite_ref-Libdesert_154-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Libdesert-154">[151]</a></sup>

The plain to the north of Uweinat is dotted with eroded volcanic features. With the discovery of oil in the 1950s also came the discovery of a massive [aquifer](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aquifer "Aquifer") underneath much of Libya. The water in the [Nubian Sandstone Aquifer System](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nubian_Sandstone_Aquifer_System "Nubian Sandstone Aquifer System") pre-dates the last [Ice ages](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_age "Ice age") and the Sahara Desert itself.<sup id="cite_ref-159"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-159">[156]</a></sup> This area also contains the [Arkenu structures](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arkenu_structures "Arkenu structures"), which were once thought to be two impact craters.<sup id="cite_ref-CigoliniOthers2012a_160-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-CigoliniOthers2012a-160">[157]</a></sup>

## Politics [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Politics")

The politics of Libya has been in a tumultuous state since the start of the [Arab Spring](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_Spring "Arab Spring") and the NATO intervention related [Libyan Crisis](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_Crisis_(2011%E2%80%93present) "Libyan Crisis (2011–present)") in 2011; the crisis resulted in the collapse of the [Libyan Arab Jamahiriya](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_Arab_Jamahiriya "Libyan Arab Jamahiriya") and the [killing](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Killing_of_Muammar_Gaddafi "Killing of Muammar Gaddafi") of [Muammar Gaddafi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi "Muammar Gaddafi"), amidst the [First Civil War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Libyan_Civil_War "First Libyan Civil War") and the [foreign military intervention](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/2011_military_intervention_in_Libya "2011 military intervention in Libya").<sup id="cite_ref-161"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-161">[158]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-162">[159]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-163">[160]</a></sup> The crisis was deepened by the [factional violence](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Factional_violence_in_Libya_(2011%E2%80%932014) "Factional violence in Libya (2011–2014)") in the [aftermath of the First Civil War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aftermath_of_the_First_Libyan_Civil_War "Aftermath of the First Libyan Civil War"), resulting in the outbreak of the [Second Civil War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Libyan_Civil_War "Second Libyan Civil War") in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-164"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-164">[161]</a></sup> The control over the country is currently split between the [House of Representatives](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Representatives_(Libya) "House of Representatives (Libya)") (HoR) in [Tobruk](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tobruk "Tobruk") and the [Government of National Unity](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_National_Unity_(Libya) "Government of National Unity (Libya)") (GNU) in [Tripoli](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tripoli,_Libya "Tripoli, Libya") and their respective supporters, as well as various [jihadist groups](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Category:Jihadist_groups_in_Libya "Category:Jihadist groups in Libya") and [tribal elements](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Libya "Ethnic groups in Libya") controlling different parts of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-165"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-165">[162]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-166">[163]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Muammar_al-Gaddafi_1-1.jpg)

[Gaddafi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaddafi "Gaddafi") was the leader of Libya until [2011 Civil War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_civil_war_(2011) "Libyan civil war (2011)").

The former legislature was the [General National Congress](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_National_Congress "General National Congress"), which had 200 seats.<sup id="cite_ref-cialegbr_167-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-cialegbr-167">[164]</a></sup> The [General National Congress (2014)](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_National_Congress_(2014) "General National Congress (2014)"), a largely unrecognised rival parliament based in the _de jure_ capital of [Tripoli](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tripoli,_Libya "Tripoli, Libya"), claims to be a legal continuation of the GNC.<sup id="cite_ref-168"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-168">[165]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-169">[166]</a></sup> On 7 July 2012, Libyans voted in [parliamentary elections](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_General_National_Congress_election,_2012 "Libyan General National Congress election, 2012"), the first free elections in almost 40 years.<sup id="cite_ref-rfi-elections_170-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-rfi-elections-170">[167]</a></sup> Around thirty women were elected to become members of parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-rfi-elections_170-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-rfi-elections-170">[167]</a></sup> Early results of the vote showed the [National Forces Alliance](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Forces_Alliance "National Forces Alliance"), led by former interim Prime Minister Mahmoud Jibril, as front runner.<sup id="cite_ref-171"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-171">[168]</a></sup> The [Justice and Construction Party](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Justice_and_Construction_Party "Justice and Construction Party"), affiliated to the [Muslim Brotherhood](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood "Muslim Brotherhood"), has done less well than similar parties in Egypt and Tunisia.<sup id="cite_ref-econ-knack_172-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-econ-knack-172">[169]</a></sup> It won 17 out of 80 seats that were contested by parties, but about 60 independents have since joined its caucus.<sup id="cite_ref-econ-knack_172-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-econ-knack-172">[169]</a></sup>

As of January 2013, there was mounting public pressure on the National Congress to set up a drafting body to create a new constitution. Congress had not yet decided whether the members of the body would be elected or appointed.<sup id="cite_ref-173"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-173">[170]</a></sup> On 30 March 2014, the General National Congress voted to replace itself with a new [House of Representatives](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Representatives_(Libya) "House of Representatives (Libya)"). The new legislature allocates 30 seats for women, will have 200 seats overall (with individuals able to run as members of political parties) and allows Libyans of foreign nationalities to run for office.<sup id="cite_ref-lh30march_174-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-lh30march-174">[171]</a></sup>

Following the 2012 elections, [Freedom House](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freedom_House "Freedom House") improved Libya's rating from Not Free to Partly Free, and now considers the country to be an electoral democracy.<sup id="cite_ref-175"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-175">[172]</a></sup> Gaddafi merged civil and [sharia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sharia#Personal_status_law_only "Sharia") courts in 1973. Civil courts now employ sharia judges who sit in regular [courts of appeal](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Court_of_appeal "Court of appeal") and specialize in sharia appellate cases.<sup id="cite_ref-176"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-176">[173]</a></sup> Laws regarding personal status are derived from Islamic law.<sup id="cite_ref-177"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-177">[174]</a></sup> At a meeting of the [European Parliament Committee on Foreign Affairs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Parliament_Committee_on_Foreign_Affairs "European Parliament Committee on Foreign Affairs") on 2 December 2014, UN Special Representative [Bernardino León](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernardino_Le%C3%B3n "Bernardino León") described Libya as a non-state.<sup id="cite_ref-178"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-178">[175]</a></sup> An agreement to form a [national unity government](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_National_Unity_(Libya) "Government of National Unity (Libya)") was signed on 17 December 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-theguardian.com_179-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-theguardian.com-179">[176]</a></sup> Under the terms of the agreement, a nine-member [Presidency Council](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidency_Council_(Libya) "Presidency Council (Libya)") and a seventeen-member interim [Government of National Accord](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_National_Accord "Government of National Accord") would be formed, with a view to holding new elections within two years.<sup id="cite_ref-theguardian.com_179-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-theguardian.com-179">[176]</a></sup> The [House of Representatives](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Representatives_(Libya) "House of Representatives (Libya)") would continue to exist as a legislature and an advisory body, to be known as the [State Council](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_Council_(Libya) "State Council (Libya)"), will be formed with members nominated by the [General National Congress (2014)](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_National_Congress_(2014) "General National Congress (2014)").<sup id="cite_ref-180"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-180">[177]</a></sup>

The formation of an interim unity government was announced on 5 February 2021, after its members were elected by the [Libyan Political Dialogue Forum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_Political_Dialogue_Forum "Libyan Political Dialogue Forum") (LPDF).<sup id="cite_ref-:0_181-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-:0-181">[178]</a></sup> Seventy four members of the LPDF cast ballots for four-member slates which would fill positions including the Prime Minister and the head of the Presidential Council.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_181-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-:0-181">[178]</a></sup> After no slates reached a 60% vote threshold, the two leading teams competed in a run-off election.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_181-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-:0-181">[178]</a></sup> [Mohamed al-Menfi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohamed_al-Menfi "Mohamed al-Menfi"), a former ambassador to Greece, became head of the Presidential Council.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_182-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-:1-182">[179]</a></sup>

Meanwhile, the LPDF confirmed that [Abdul Hamid Dbeibeh](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdul_Hamid_Dbeibeh "Abdul Hamid Dbeibeh"), a businessman, would be the transitional Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_182-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-:1-182">[179]</a></sup> All of the candidates who ran in this election, including the members of the winning slate, promised to appoint women to 30% of all senior government positions.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_182-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-:1-182">[179]</a></sup> The politicians elected to lead the interim government initially agreed not to stand in the national elections scheduled for 24 December 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_182-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-:1-182">[179]</a></sup> However, Abdul Hamid Dbeibeh announced his candidature for president despite the ban in November 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-183"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-183">[180]</a></sup> The Appeals Court in Tripoli rejected appeals for his disqualification, and allowed Dbeibeh back on the candidates' list, along with a number of other disqualified candidates, originally scheduled for December 24.<sup id="cite_ref-184"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-184">[181]</a></sup>

Even more controversially, the court also reinstated [Saif al-Islam Gaddafi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saif_al-Islam_Gaddafi "Saif al-Islam Gaddafi"), a son of the former dictator, as a presidential candidate.<sup id="cite_ref-185"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-185">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-186">[183]</a></sup> On 22 December 2021, Libya's Election Commission called for the postponement of the election until 24 January 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-187"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-187">[184]</a></sup> Earlier, a parliamentary commission said it would be "impossible" to hold the election on 24 December 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_188-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-ReferenceA-188">[185]</a></sup> The UN called on Libya's interim leaders to "expeditiously address all legal and political obstacles to hold elections, including finalising the list of presidential candidates".<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_188-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-ReferenceA-188">[185]</a></sup> However, at the last minute, the election was postponed indefinitely and the international community agreed to continue its support and recognition of the interim government headed by Mr Dbeibeh.<sup id="cite_ref-189"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-189">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-190">[187]</a></sup> According to new election rules, a new prime minister has 21 days to form a cabinet that must be endorsed by the various governing bodies within Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_182-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-:1-182">[179]</a></sup> After this cabinet is agreed upon, the unity government will replace all "parallel authorities" within Libya, including the Government of National Accord in Tripoli and the administration led by General Haftar.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_182-5"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-:1-182">[179]</a></sup>

### Foreign relations [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Foreign relations")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Secretary_Kerry,_UK_Foreign_Secretary_Hague_Hold_News_Conference_With_Libyan_Prime_Minister_Ziedan_(11035773394).jpg)

UK Foreign Secretary [William Hague](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Hague "William Hague") with Libyan Prime Minister [Ali Zeidan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ali_Zeidan "Ali Zeidan") and U.S. Secretary of State [John Kerry](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Kerry "John Kerry"), November 2013

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mutassim_Gadaffi_Hillary_Clinton.jpg)

Libyan National Security Advisor [Mutassim Gaddafi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutassim_Gaddafi "Mutassim Gaddafi") and U.S. Secretary of State [Hillary Clinton](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hillary_Clinton "Hillary Clinton"), April 2009

Libya's foreign policies have fluctuated since 1951. As a Kingdom, Libya maintained a definitively pro-Western stance, and was recognized as belonging to the conservative traditionalist bloc in the League of Arab States (the present-day [Arab League](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_League "Arab League")), of which it became a member in 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-191"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-191">[188]</a></sup> The government was also friendly towards Western countries such as the United Kingdom, United States, [France](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_Fifth_Republic "French Fifth Republic"), [Italy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Italian_Republic "First Italian Republic"), [Greece](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Greece_(Gl%C3%BCcksburg) "Kingdom of Greece (Glücksburg)"), and established full diplomatic relations with the [Soviet Union](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union "Soviet Union") in 1955.<sup id="cite_ref-192"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-192">[189]</a></sup> Although the government supported Arab causes, including the Moroccan and Algerian independence movements, it took little active part in the [Arab-Israeli dispute](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab%E2%80%93Israeli_conflict "Arab–Israeli conflict") or the tumultuous inter-Arab politics of the 1950s and early 1960s. The Kingdom was noted for its close association with the West, while it steered a conservative course at home.<sup id="cite_ref-193"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-193">[190]</a></sup>

After the 1969 [coup](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat "Coup d'état"), [Muammar Gaddafi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi "Muammar Gaddafi") closed American and British bases and partly [nationalized](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nationalization "Nationalization") foreign oil and commercial interests in Libya. Gaddafi was known for backing a number of leaders viewed as anathema to [Westernization](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Westernization "Westernization") and [political liberalism](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_liberalism "Political liberalism"), including [Ugandan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Uganda_(1971%E2%80%9379) "History of Uganda (1971–79)") President [Idi Amin](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Idi_Amin "Idi Amin"),<sup id="cite_ref-194"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-194">[191]</a></sup> [Central African](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_African_Empire "Central African Empire") Emperor [Jean-Bédel Bokassa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean-B%C3%A9del_Bokassa "Jean-Bédel Bokassa"),<sup id="cite_ref-stanik23_195-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-stanik23-195">[192]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-leedavis16_196-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-leedavis16-196">[193]</a></sup> [Ethiopian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derg "Derg") strongman [Haile Mariam Mengistu](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haile_Mariam_Mengistu "Haile Mariam Mengistu"),<sup id="cite_ref-leedavis16_196-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-leedavis16-196">[193]</a></sup> Liberian President [Charles Taylor](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Taylor_(Liberian_politician) "Charles Taylor (Liberian politician)"),<sup id="cite_ref-economistfall_197-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-economistfall-197">[194]</a></sup> and [Yugoslav](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serbia_and_Montenegro/History "Serbia and Montenegro/History") President [Slobodan Milošević](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slobodan_Milo%C5%A1evi%C4%87 "Slobodan Milošević").<sup id="cite_ref-198"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-198">[195]</a></sup>

Relations with the West were strained by a series of incidents for most of Gaddafi's rule,<sup id="cite_ref-199"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-199">[196]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated183_200-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-autogenerated183-200">[197]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-201">[198]</a></sup> including the killing of [London](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/City_of_London_Police "City of London Police") policewoman [Yvonne Fletcher](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yvonne_Fletcher "Yvonne Fletcher"), the [bombing](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1986_Berlin_discotheque_bombing "1986 Berlin discotheque bombing") of a [West Berlin](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Berlin "West Berlin") nightclub frequented by U.S. servicemen, and the bombing of [Pan Am Flight 103](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pan_Am_Flight_103 "Pan Am Flight 103"), which led to UN sanctions in the 1990s, though by the late 2000s, the United States and other Western powers had normalised relations with Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-SalakLibya_63-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-SalakLibya-63">[60]</a></sup> Gaddafi's decision to abandon the pursuit of [weapons of mass destruction](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weapons_of_mass_destruction "Weapons of mass destruction") after the [Iraq War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iraq_War "Iraq War") saw [Iraqi dictator](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Iraq_under_Ba%27athist_rule "History of Iraq under Ba'athist rule") [Saddam Hussein](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saddam_Hussein "Saddam Hussein") overthrown and put on trial led to Libya being hailed as a success for Western [soft power](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soft_power "Soft power") initiatives in the [War on Terror](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_on_Terror "War on Terror").<sup id="cite_ref-202"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-202">[199]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-203">[200]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-204">[201]</a></sup> In October 2010, Gaddafi apologized to African leaders on behalf of Arab nations for their involvement in the [trans-Saharan slave trade](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trans-Saharan_slave_trade "Trans-Saharan slave trade").<sup id="cite_ref-France_24_2010_205-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-France_24_2010-205">[202]</a></sup>

Libya is included in the European Union's [European Neighbourhood Policy](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Neighbourhood_Policy "European Neighbourhood Policy") (ENP) which aims at bringing the EU and its neighbours closer. Libyan authorities rejected European Union's plans aimed at stopping [migration from Libya](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immigration_to_Europe "Immigration to Europe").<sup id="cite_ref-206"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-206">[203]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-207">[204]</a></sup> In 2017, Libya signed the UN [treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_on_the_Prohibition_of_Nuclear_Weapons "Treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons").<sup id="cite_ref-208"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-208">[205]</a></sup>

### Military [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=16 "Edit section: Military")

Learn more

This article needs to be **updated**. _(April 2016)_

Libya's [previous national army](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armed_Forces_of_the_Libyan_Arab_Jamahiriya "Armed Forces of the Libyan Arab Jamahiriya") was defeated in the [Libyan Civil War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Libyan_Civil_War "First Libyan Civil War") and disbanded. The [Tobruk](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tobruk "Tobruk") based [House of Representatives](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Representatives_(Libya) "House of Representatives (Libya)") who claim to be the legitimate government of Libya have attempted to reestablish a military known as the [Libyan National Army](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_National_Army "Libyan National Army"). Led by [Khalifa Haftar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khalifa_Haftar "Khalifa Haftar"), they control much of eastern Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-209"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-209">[206]</a></sup> In May 2012, an estimated 35,000 personnel had joined its ranks.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_210-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-nytimes-210">[207]</a></sup> The internationally recognised [Government of National Accord](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_National_Accord "Government of National Accord") established in 2015 has [its own army](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_Army "Libyan Army") that replaced the LNA, but it consists largely of undisciplined and disorganised militia groups.

As of November 2012, it was deemed to be still in the embryonic stage of development.<sup id="cite_ref-211"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-211">[208]</a></sup> President [Mohammed el-Megarif](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammed_Magariaf "Mohammed Magariaf") promised that empowering the army and police force is the government's biggest priority.<sup id="cite_ref-212"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-212">[209]</a></sup> President el-Megarif also ordered that all of the country's militias must come under government authority or disband.<sup id="cite_ref-213"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-213">[210]</a></sup>

Militias have so far refused to be integrated into a central security force.<sup id="cite_ref-hangover_214-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-hangover-214">[211]</a></sup> Many of these militias are disciplined, but the most powerful of them answer only to the executive councils of various Libyan cities.<sup id="cite_ref-hangover_214-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-hangover-214">[211]</a></sup> These militias make up the so-called [Libyan Shield](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya_Shield_Force "Libya Shield Force"), a parallel national force, which operates at the request, rather than at the order, of the defence ministry.<sup id="cite_ref-hangover_214-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-hangover-214">[211]</a></sup>

### Administrative divisions [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Administrative divisions")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Libya,_administrative_divisions_-_Nmbrs_-_colored.svg)

Districts of Libya since 2007

Historically, the area of Libya was considered three provinces (or states), [Tripolitania](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tripolitania "Tripolitania") in the northwest, [Barka (Cyrenaica)](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyrenaica "Cyrenaica") in the east, and [Fezzan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fezzan "Fezzan") in the southwest. It was the conquest by Italy in the [Italo-Turkish War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italo-Turkish_War "Italo-Turkish War") that united them in a single political unit.

Since 2007, Libya has been divided into 22 districts ([Shabiyat](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shabiyat "Shabiyat")):

1.  [Nuqat al Khams](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuqat_al_Khams "Nuqat al Khams")
2.  [Zawiya](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zawiya_District "Zawiya District")
3.  [Jafara](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jafara "Jafara")
4.  [Tripoli](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tripoli_District,_Libya "Tripoli District, Libya")
5.  [Murqub](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Murqub_District "Murqub District")
6.  [Misrata](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Misrata_District "Misrata District")
7.  [Sirte](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sirte_District "Sirte District")
8.  [Benghazi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benghazi_District "Benghazi District")
9.  [Marj](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marj_District "Marj District")
10.  [Jabal al Akhdar](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jabal_al_Akhdar "Jabal al Akhdar")
11.  [Derna](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derna_District "Derna District")
12.  [Tobruk](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tobruk_District "Tobruk District")
13.  [Nalut](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nalut_District "Nalut District")
14.  [Jabal al Gharbi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jabal_al_Gharbi_District "Jabal al Gharbi District")
15.  [Wadi al Shatii](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wadi_al_Shatii_District "Wadi al Shatii District")
16.  [Jufra](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jufra_District "Jufra District")
17.  [Al Wahat](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al_Wahat_District "Al Wahat District")
18.  [Ghat](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghat_District "Ghat District")
19.  [Wadi al Hayaa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wadi_al_Hayaa_District "Wadi al Hayaa District")
20.  [Sabha](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sabha_District "Sabha District")
21.  [Murzuq](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Murzuq_District "Murzuq District")
22.  [Kufra](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kufra_District "Kufra District")

In 2022, 18 provinces were declared by the Libyan Government of National Unity ([Libyan Observer](https://web.archive.org/web/20220302150300/https://www.libyaobserver.ly/news/libyan-government-divides-country-18-provinces)): the eastern coast, Jabal Al-Akhdar, Al-Hizam, Benghazi, Al-Wahat, Al-Kufra, Al-Khaleej, Al-Margab, Tripoli, Al-Jafara, Al-Zawiya, West Coast, Gheryan, Zintan, Nalut, Sabha, Al-Wadi, and Murzuq Basin.

### Human rights [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Human rights")

According to [Human Rights Watch](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch "Human Rights Watch") annual report 2016, journalists are still being targeted by the armed groups in Libya. The organization added that Libya ranked very low in the 2015 [Press Freedom Index](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Press_Freedom_Index "Press Freedom Index"), 154th out of 180 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-215"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-215">[212]</a></sup> For the 2021 Press Freedom Index its score dropped to 165th out of 180 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-216"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-216">[213]</a></sup> [Homosexuality](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Libya "LGBT rights in Libya") is illegal in Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-217"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-217">[214]</a></sup>

## Economy [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Economy")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:GDP_per_capita_development_in_Libya.svg)

Change in per capita GDP of Libya, 1950–2018. Figures are inflation-adjusted to 2011 International dollars.

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Libya_Product_Exports_(2019).svg)

A proportional representation of Libya exports, 2019

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tripolitowers.jpg)

A view of the Business District in [Tripoli](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tripoli,_Libya "Tripoli, Libya")

The Libyan economy depends primarily upon revenues from the [oil sector](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petroleum_industry "Petroleum industry"), which account for over half of GDP and 97% of exports.<sup id="cite_ref-star-oil_218-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-star-oil-218">[215]</a></sup> Libya holds the largest proven oil reserves in Africa and is an important contributor to the global supply of light, [sweet crude](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sweet_crude "Sweet crude").<sup id="cite_ref-eia-libya_219-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-eia-libya-219">[216]</a></sup> During 2010, when oil averaged at $80 a barrel, oil production accounted for 54% of GDP.<sup id="cite_ref-220"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-220">[217]</a></sup> Apart from petroleum, the other natural resources are natural gas and [gypsum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gypsum "Gypsum").<sup id="cite_ref-opec-libya_221-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-opec-libya-221">[218]</a></sup> The [International Monetary Fund](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund "International Monetary Fund") estimated Libya's real GDP growth at 122% in 2012 and 16.7% in 2013, after a 60% plunge in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-star-oil_218-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-star-oil-218">[215]</a></sup>

The [World Bank](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Bank "World Bank") defines Libya as an 'Upper Middle Income Economy', along with only seven other African countries.<sup id="cite_ref-222"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-222">[219]</a></sup> Substantial revenues from the energy sector, coupled with a small population, give Libya one of the highest per capita GDPs in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-opec-libya_221-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-opec-libya-221">[218]</a></sup> This allowed the [Libyan Arab Jamahiriya](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_Arab_Jamahiriya "Libyan Arab Jamahiriya") state to provide an extensive level of [social security](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_security "Social security"), particularly in the fields of housing and education.<sup id="cite_ref-223"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-223">[220]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bouri_NC_41_DP4_platform.jpg)

An [oil platform](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oil_platform "Oil platform") off the Libyan coast

Libya faces many structural problems including a lack of institutions, weak governance, and chronic [structural unemployment](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Structural_unemployment "Structural unemployment").<sup id="cite_ref-imf-survey_224-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-imf-survey-224">[221]</a></sup> The economy displays a lack of economic diversification and significant reliance on immigrant labour.<sup id="cite_ref-ilo-libya_225-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-ilo-libya-225">[222]</a></sup> Libya has traditionally relied on unsustainably high levels of public sector hiring to create employment.<sup id="cite_ref-african-outlook_226-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-african-outlook-226">[223]</a></sup> In the mid-2000s, the government employed about 70% of all national employees.<sup id="cite_ref-ilo-libya_225-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-ilo-libya-225">[222]</a></sup>

Unemployment rose from 8% in 2008 to 21% in 2009, according to the census figures.<sup id="cite_ref-227"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-227">[224]</a></sup> According to an [Arab League](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_League "Arab League") report, based on data from 2010, unemployment for women stands at 18% while for the figure for men is 21%, making Libya the only Arab country where there are more unemployed men than women.<sup id="cite_ref-228"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-228">[225]</a></sup> Libya has high levels of social inequality, high rates of youth unemployment and regional economic disparities.<sup id="cite_ref-african-outlook_226-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-african-outlook-226">[223]</a></sup> Water supply is also a problem, with some 28% of the population not having access to safe drinking water in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-229"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-229">[226]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Libyan_pivot_irrigation_460142568_02e969004a_o.jpg)

[Pivot irrigation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pivot_irrigation "Pivot irrigation") in [Kufra](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kufra "Kufra"), southeast Libya, 2008

Two trans-African automobile routes pass through Libya, which includes [Cairo-Dakar Highway](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cairo-Dakar_Highway "Cairo-Dakar Highway") and [Tripoli-Cape Town Highway](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tripoli-Cape_Town_Highway "Tripoli-Cape Town Highway"). These routes have further contributed in the economic development of Libya.

Libya imports up to 90% of its cereal consumption requirements, and imports of wheat in 2012/13 was estimated at 1 million tonnes.<sup id="cite_ref-fao_giews_230-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-fao_giews-230">[227]</a></sup> The 2012 wheat production was estimated at 200,000 tonnes.<sup id="cite_ref-fao_giews_230-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-fao_giews-230">[227]</a></sup> The government hopes to increase food production to 800,000 tonnes of cereals by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-fao_giews_230-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-fao_giews-230">[227]</a></sup> However, natural and environmental conditions limit Libya's agricultural production potential.<sup id="cite_ref-fao_giews_230-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-fao_giews-230">[227]</a></sup> Before 1958, agriculture was the country's main source of revenue, making up about 30% of GDP. With the discovery of oil in 1958, the size of the agriculture sector declined rapidly, comprising less than 5% GDP by 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-voa-olive_231-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-voa-olive-231">[228]</a></sup>

The country joined [OPEC](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/OPEC "OPEC") in 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-opec-libya_221-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-opec-libya-221">[218]</a></sup> Libya is not a [WTO](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/WTO "WTO") member, but negotiations for its accession started in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-232"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-232">[229]</a></sup> In the early 1980s, Libya was one of the wealthiest countries in the world; its [GDP per capita](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/GDP_per_capita "GDP per capita") was higher than some developed countries.<sup id="cite_ref-233"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-233">[230]</a></sup>

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Libya_location_map-oil_%26_gas_2011-en.svg)

Oil is the major natural resource of Libya, with [estimated reserves of 43.6 billion barrels](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oil_reserves_in_Libya "Oil reserves in Libya").<sup id="cite_ref-234"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-234">[231]</a></sup>

In the early 2000s officials of the Jamahiriya era carried out economic reforms to reintegrate Libya into the global economy.<sup id="cite_ref-spooks_235-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-spooks-235">[232]</a></sup> [UN sanctions](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_sanctions "International sanctions") were lifted in September 2003, and Libya announced in December 2003 that it would abandon programs to build weapons of mass destruction.<sup>[<i><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Other steps have included applying for membership of the [World Trade Organization](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Trade_Organization "World Trade Organization"), reducing [subsidies](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subsidy "Subsidy"), and announcing plans for [privatization](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Privatization "Privatization").<sup id="cite_ref-236"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-236">[233]</a></sup>

Authorities privatized more than 100 government owned companies after 2003 in industries including oil refining, tourism and real estate, of which 29 were 100% foreign owned.<sup id="cite_ref-237"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-237">[234]</a></sup> Many international oil companies returned to the country, including oil giants [Shell](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell "Royal Dutch Shell") and [ExxonMobil](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/ExxonMobil "ExxonMobil").<sup id="cite_ref-238"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-238">[235]</a></sup> After sanctions were lifted there was a gradual increase of air traffic, and by 2005 there were 1.5 million yearly air travellers.<sup id="cite_ref-239"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-239">[236]</a></sup> Libya had long been a notoriously difficult country for Western tourists to visit due to stringent visa requirements.<sup id="cite_ref-240"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-240">[237]</a></sup>

In 2007 [Saif al-Islam Gaddafi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saif_al-Islam_Gaddafi "Saif al-Islam Gaddafi"), the second-eldest son of Muammar Gaddafi, was involved in a [green development](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green_development "Green development") project called the Green Mountain Sustainable Development Area, which sought to bring tourism to [Cyrene](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyrene,_Libya "Cyrene, Libya") and to preserve [Greek](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_Greece "Classical Greece") ruins in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-241"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-241">[238]</a></sup> On August 2011 it was estimated that it would take at least 10 years to rebuild Libya's infrastructure. Even before the 2011 war, Libya's infrastructure was in a poor state due to "utter neglect" by Gaddafi's administration, according to the NTC.<sup id="cite_ref-242"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-242">[239]</a></sup> By October 2012, the economy had recovered from the 2011 conflict, with oil production returning to near normal levels.<sup id="cite_ref-star-oil_218-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-star-oil-218">[215]</a></sup> Oil production was more than 1.6 million barrels per day before the war. By October 2012, the average oil production has surpassed 1.4 million bpd.<sup id="cite_ref-star-oil_218-3"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-star-oil-218">[215]</a></sup> The resumption of production was made possible due to the quick return of major Western companies, like [TotalEnergies](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/TotalEnergies "TotalEnergies"), [Eni](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eni "Eni"), [Repsol](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Repsol "Repsol"), [Wintershall](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wintershall "Wintershall") and [Occidental](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occidental_Petroleum "Occidental Petroleum").<sup id="cite_ref-star-oil_218-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-star-oil-218">[215]</a></sup> In 2016, an announcement from the company said the company aims 900,000 barrel per day in the next year. Oil production has fallen from 1.6 million barrel per day to 900,000 in four years of war.<sup id="cite_ref-243"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-243">[240]</a></sup>

The [Great Man-Made River](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Man-Made_River "Great Man-Made River") is the world's largest [irrigation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irrigation "Irrigation") project.<sup id="cite_ref-244"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-244">[241]</a></sup> The project utilizes a pipeline system that pumps [fossil water](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossil_water "Fossil water") from the [Nubian Sandstone Aquifer System](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nubian_Sandstone_Aquifer_System "Nubian Sandstone Aquifer System") from down south in Libya to cities in the populous Libyan northern Mediterranean coast including Tripoli and Benghazi. The water provides 70% of all freshwater used in Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-245"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-245">[242]</a></sup> During the second Libyan civil war, lasting from 2014 to 2020, the water infrastructure suffered neglect and occasional breakdowns.<sup id="cite_ref-246"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-246">[243]</a></sup> By 2017, 60% of the Libyan population were [malnourished](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malnutrition "Malnutrition"). Since then, 1.3 million people are waiting for emergency humanitarian aid, out of a total population of 7.1 million.<sup id="cite_ref-247"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-247">[244]</a></sup>

In March 2024, Libya is actively promoting business development and encouraging both domestic and foreign investment. This strategic initiative is aimed at securing long-term economic stability and prosperity for Libya by diversifying its economic foundation, decreasing reliance on a singular sector, and establishing resilience in the face of fluctuating oil markets. Embracing green industries like renewable energy, energy efficiency, sustainable agriculture, and eco-tourism holds the potential to generate fresh employment prospects across a spectrum of sectors, thereby addressing unemployment challenges, particularly among the youth demographic.<sup id="cite_ref-248"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-248">[245]</a></sup>

## Demographics [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=20 "Edit section: Demographics")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Libya_ethnic.svg)

Ethnic composition of the Libyan population in 1974 (CIA map)

  Uninhabited

Libya is a large country with a relatively small population concentrated very narrowly along the coast.<sup id="cite_ref-249"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-249">[246]</a></sup> Its population density is about 50 inhabitants per square kilometre (130/sq mi) in the two northern regions of [Tripolitania](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tripolitania "Tripolitania") and [Cyrenaica](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyrenaica "Cyrenaica"), but falls to less than 1 inhabitant per square kilometre (2.6/sq mi) elsewhere. Ninety percent of the people live along the coast in less than 10% of the area.

About 88% of the population is urban, mostly concentrated in the three largest cities, [Tripoli](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tripoli,_Libya "Tripoli, Libya"), [Benghazi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benghazi "Benghazi") and [Misrata](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Misrata "Misrata"). Libya has a population of about 6.7 million,<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_250-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-UN_WPP-250">[247]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2022_251-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-UN_WPP_2022-251">[248]</a></sup> 27.7% of whom are under the age of 15.<sup id="cite_ref-spooks_235-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-spooks-235">[232]</a></sup> In 1984 the population was 3.6 million, an increase from the 1.54 million reported in 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-252"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-252">[249]</a></sup>

The population of Libya is primarily of [Arab](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs "Arabs") ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-253"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-253">[250]</a></sup> Arabs comprise 92% of the population, while [Berbers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Berbers "Berbers") comprise 5%.<sup id="cite_ref-254"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-254">[251]</a></sup> Unofficial estimates put the number of Berbers in Libya at around 600,000, about 10% of the population of Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-255"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-255">[252]</a></sup> Among the Berber groups are the minority Berber populations of [Zuwarah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zuwarah "Zuwarah") and the [Nafusa Mountains](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nafusa_Mountains "Nafusa Mountains"). Southern Libya, primarily [Sebha](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sebha "Sebha"), [Kufra](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kufra "Kufra"), [Ghat](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghat,_Libya "Ghat, Libya"), [Ghadamis](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghadamis "Ghadamis") and [Murzuk](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Murzuk "Murzuk"), is also inhabited by two other ethnic groups; the [Tuareg](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tuareg_people "Tuareg people") and [Toubou](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toubou_people "Toubou people"). Libya is one of the world's most [tribal](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tribalism "Tribalism") countries. There are about 140 tribes and [clans](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clan "Clan") in Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-256"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-256">[253]</a></sup> Additionally, there are an estimated 750,000 [Egyptian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egyptians "Egyptians") workers residing in Libya, which had declined from the 2 million Egyptians in Libya prior to the [2011 Civil War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Libyan_Civil_War "First Libyan Civil War") that led to the overthrow of Libyan leader [Muammar Gaddafi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi "Muammar Gaddafi").<sup id="cite_ref-257"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-257">[254]</a></sup>

Family life is important for Libyan families, the majority of whom live in [apartment blocks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tower_block "Tower block") and other independent housing units, with modes of housing depending on their income and wealth. Although the Arab Libyans traditionally lived nomadic [Bedouin](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bedouin "Bedouin") lifestyles in tents, they have predominately settled in towns and cities.<sup id="cite_ref-258"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-258">[255]</a></sup>

Because of this, their old ways of life are gradually fading out. An unknown small number of Libyans still live in the desert as their families have done for centuries. Most of the population has occupations in industry and [services](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tertiary_sector_of_the_economy "Tertiary sector of the economy"), and a small percentage is in agriculture.

According to the UNHCR, there were around 8,000 registered refugees, 5,500 unregistered refugees, and 7,000 asylum seekers of various origins in Libya in January 2013. Additionally, 47,000 Libyan nationals were internally displaced and 46,570 were internally displaced returnees.<sup id="cite_ref-259"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-259">[256]</a></sup>

### Health [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: Health")

In 2010, spending on healthcare accounted for 3.88% of the country's GDP. In 2009, there were 18.71 physicians and 66.95 nurses per 10,000 inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-260"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-260">[257]</a></sup> The life expectancy at birth was 74.95 years in 2011, or 72.44 years for males and 77.59 years for females.<sup id="cite_ref-261"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-261">[258]</a></sup>

In 2023, the Libyan health ministry announced the launch of the National Strategy for Primary Healthcare 2023–2028 to improve services provided by group clinics and health centers. A unique health number allotted to each citizen will facilitate access to medical records. Before Storm Daniel, the UN Office for the Coordination of Humanitarian Affairs estimated that around 60,000 people were in need of humanitarian aid in Derna and environs. Since the storm many of the hospitals and primary health facilities in Derna and eastern Libya have been rendered partially or completely nonfunctional.<sup id="cite_ref-262"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-262">[259]</a></sup>

### Education [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=22 "Edit section: Education")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Piazza_28_Ottobre,_Bengasi.jpg)

Al Manar Royal Palace in central Benghazi – the location of the [University of Libya](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Libya "University of Libya")'s first campus, founded by royal decree in 1955

Libya's population includes 1.7 million students, over 270,000 of whom study at the [tertiary level](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tertiary_education "Tertiary education").<sup id="cite_ref-Libedu_263-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Libedu-263">[260]</a></sup> Basic education in Libya is free for all citizens,<sup id="cite_ref-264"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-264">[261]</a></sup> and is compulsory up to the [secondary level](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secondary_education "Secondary education"). The adult literacy rate in 2010 was 89.2%.<sup id="cite_ref-unescolit_265-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-unescolit-265">[262]</a></sup>

After Libya's independence in 1951, its first university – the [University of Libya](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Libya "University of Libya") – was established in Benghazi by royal decree.<sup id="cite_ref-Libedu2_266-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Libedu2-266">[263]</a></sup> In the 1975–76 academic year the number of university students was estimated to be 13,418. As of 2004, this number has increased to more than 200,000, with another 70,000 enrolled in the higher technical and vocational sector.<sup id="cite_ref-Libedu_263-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Libedu-263">[260]</a></sup> The rapid increase in the number of students in the higher education sector has been mirrored by an increase in the number of institutions of higher education.

Since 1975 the number of public universities has grown from two to twelve and since their introduction in 1980, the number of higher technical and vocational institutes has grown to 84.<sup>[<i>? <a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2012)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Libedu_263-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Libedu-263">[260]</a></sup> Since 2007 some new private universities such as the [Libyan International Medical University](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_International_Medical_University "Libyan International Medical University") have been established. Although before 2011 a small number of private institutions were given accreditation, the majority of Libya's higher education has always been financed by the public budget. In 1998 the budget allocation for education represented 38.2% of Libya's national budget.<sup id="cite_ref-Libedu2_266-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Libedu2-266">[263]</a></sup>

In 2024, the Ministry of Education announced the launch of the Full-Day School Project in which 12 schools in different parts of the country will have longer school days. The project aims to provide 800 hours of instruction per year to 3,300 elementary school students.<sup id="cite_ref-267"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-267">[264]</a></sup>

### Ethnicity [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=23 "Edit section: Ethnicity")

The original inhabitants of Libya belonged predominantly to [Berber](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Berber_people "Berber people") ethnic groups; however, the long series of foreign invasions and migrations – particularly by [Arabs](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs "Arabs") – had a profound and lasting ethnic, linguistic, and cultural influence on Libyan demographics. Centuries of large-scale [Arab migration to the Maghreb](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_migration_to_the_Maghreb "Arab migration to the Maghreb") since the 7th century shifted the demographics of Libya in favor of Arabs. Some [Turks](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkish_people "Turkish people") settled in Libya during the rule of the [Ottoman Empire](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Empire "Ottoman Empire").

Most of Libya's inhabitants are [Arab](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabs "Arabs"),<sup id="cite_ref-:2_19-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-:2-19">[16]</a></sup> with many tracing their ancestry to Bedouin Arab tribes like [Banu Sulaym](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Banu_Sulaym "Banu Sulaym") and [Banu Hilal](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Banu_Hilal "Banu Hilal"), plus Turkish and Berber minorities. The [Turkish minority](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turks_in_Libya "Turks in Libya") are often called "[Kouloughlis](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kouloughlis "Kouloughlis")" and are concentrated in and around villages and towns.<sup id="cite_ref-268"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-268">[265]</a></sup> There are some ethnic minorities, such as the Berber [Tuareg](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tuareg_people "Tuareg people") and the Black African [Tebou](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toubou_people "Toubou people").<sup id="cite_ref-Dupree_1958_loc=33-44_269-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Dupree_1958_loc=33-44-269">[266]</a></sup>

Most [Italian settlers](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italian_settlers_in_Libya "Italian settlers in Libya"), at their height numbering over half a million, left after Italian Libya's independence in 1947. More repatriated in 1970 after the accession of Muammar Gaddafi, but a few hundred returned in the 2000s.<sup id="cite_ref-270"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-270">[267]</a></sup>

### Foreign Labour [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=24 "Edit section: Foreign Labour")

As of 2023 the IOM estimates that approximately 10% of Libya's population (upwards of 700,000 people) constituted foreign labour.<sup id="cite_ref-271"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-271">[268]</a></sup> Prior to the 2011 revolution, official and unofficial figures of migrant labour ranged from 25% to 40% of the population (between 1.5 and 2.4 million people). Historically, Libya hosted millions of low- and high-skilled Egyptian migrants, in particular.<sup id="cite_ref-272"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-272">[269]</a></sup>

It is difficult to estimate the total number of immigrants in Libya because the census figures, official counts and typically more accurate unofficial estimates all differ. In the 2006 census, around 359,540 foreign nationals were resident in Libya out of a population of over 5.5 million (6.35% of the population). Almost half of these were Egyptians, followed by Sudanese and Palestinian immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-EU_migrant_policy_Centre_273-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-EU_migrant_policy_Centre-273">[270]</a></sup> During the 2011 revolution, 768,362 immigrants fled Libya as calculated by the [IOM](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Organization_for_Migration "International Organization for Migration"), around 13% of the population at the time, although many more stayed on in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-EU_migrant_policy_Centre_273-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-EU_migrant_policy_Centre-273">[270]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-274"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-274">[271]</a></sup>

If consular records prior to the revolution are used to estimate the immigrant population, as many as 2 million Egyptian migrants were recorded by the Egyptian embassy in Tripoli in 2009, followed by 87,200 Tunisians, and 68,200 Moroccans by their respective embassies. Turkey recorded the evacuation of 25,000 workers during the 2011 uprising.<sup id="cite_ref-275"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-275">[272]</a></sup> The number of Asian migrants before the revolution were just over 100,000 (60,000 Bangladeshis, 20,000 Filipinos, 18,000 Indians, 10,000 Pakistanis, as well as Chinese, Korean, Vietnamese, Thai and other workers).<sup id="cite_ref-276"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-276">[273]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-277"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-277">[274]</a></sup> This would put the immigrant population at almost 40% before the revolution and is a figure more consistent with government estimates in 2004 which put the regular and irregular migrant numbers at 1.35 to 1.8 million (25–33% of the population at the time).<sup id="cite_ref-EU_migrant_policy_Centre_273-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-EU_migrant_policy_Centre-273">[270]</a></sup>

Libya's native population of Arabs-Berbers as well as Arab migrants of various nationalities collectively make up 97% of the population as of 2014.

### Languages [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=25 "Edit section: Languages")

According to the [CIA](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/CIA "CIA"), the official language of Libya is Arabic.<sup id="cite_ref-Cfbly_278-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Cfbly-278">[275]</a></sup> The local [Libyan Arabic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libyan_Arabic "Libyan Arabic") variety is spoken alongside [Modern Standard Arabic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic "Modern Standard Arabic"). Various [Berber languages](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Berber_languages "Berber languages") are also spoken, including [Tamasheq](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tamasheq_language "Tamasheq language"), [Ghadamis](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghadamis "Ghadamis"), [Nafusi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nafusi "Nafusi"), [Suknah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suknah "Suknah") and [Awjilah](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Awjilah_(language) "Awjilah (language)").<sup id="cite_ref-Cfbly_278-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Cfbly-278">[275]</a></sup> The Libyan Amazigh High Council (LAHC) has declared the Amazigh ([Berber](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Berbers "Berbers") or Tamazight) language to be official in the cities and districts inhabited by the Berbers in Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-279"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-279">[276]</a></sup> In addition, [English](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_language "English language") is widely understood in the major cities,<sup id="cite_ref-280"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-280">[277]</a></sup> while the former colonial language of [Italian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italian_language "Italian language") is also used in commerce and by the remaining Italian population.<sup id="cite_ref-Cfbly_278-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Cfbly-278">[275]</a></sup>

### Religion [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=26 "Edit section: Religion")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ghadames_-_Grosse_Moschee.jpg)

Mosque in [Ghadames](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghadames "Ghadames"), close to the Tunisian and Algerian border
