About 97% of the population in Libya are [Muslims](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslims "Muslims"), most of whom belong to the [Sunni branch](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sunni_Islam "Sunni Islam").<sup id="cite_ref-spooks_235-2"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-spooks-235">[232]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chivvis-2014_281-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Chivvis-2014-281">[278]</a></sup> Small numbers of [Ibadi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibadi "Ibadi") Muslims live in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-282"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-282">[279]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-283"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-283">[280]</a></sup>

Before the 1930s, the [Senussi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Senussi "Senussi") Sunni Sufi movement was the primary Islamic movement in Libya. This was a religious revival adapted to desert life. Its _zawaaya_ (lodges) were found in [Tripolitania](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tripolitania "Tripolitania") and [Fezzan](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fezzan "Fezzan"), but Senussi influence was strongest in [Cyrenaica](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyrenaica "Cyrenaica"). Rescuing the region from unrest and anarchy, the Senussi movement gave the Cyrenaican tribal people a religious attachment and feelings of unity and purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-senussi_284-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-senussi-284">[281]</a></sup> This Islamic movement was eventually destroyed by the [Italian invasion](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italo-Turkish_War "Italo-Turkish War"). Gaddafi asserted that he was a devout Muslim, and his government was taking a role in supporting Islamic institutions and in worldwide proselytising on behalf of Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-285"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-285">[282]</a></sup>

The [International Religious Freedom Report](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Religious_Freedom_Report "International Religious Freedom Report") 2004 noted that "bishops, priests and nuns wear religious dress freely in public and report virtually no discrimination," while also "enjoying good relations with the Government". The report also indicated that members of minority religions said "they do not face harassment by authorities or the Muslim majority on the basis of their religious practices". The [International Christian Concern](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Christian_Concern "International Christian Concern") does not list Libya as a country where there is "persecution or severe discrimination against Christians".<sup id="cite_ref-286"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-286">[283]</a></sup>

Since the [fall of Gaddafi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Libyan_Civil_War "First Libyan Civil War"), ultra-conservative strains of Islam have reasserted themselves in places. [Derna](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derna,_Libya "Derna, Libya") in eastern Libya, historically a hotbed of [jihadist](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jihad "Jihad") thought, came under the control of militants aligned with the [Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant "Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant") in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-287"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-287">[284]</a></sup> Jihadist elements have also spread to [Sirte](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sirte "Sirte") and [Benghazi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benghazi "Benghazi"), among other areas, as a result of the [Second Libyan Civil War](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Libyan_Civil_War_(2014%E2%80%93present) "Second Libyan Civil War (2014–present)").<sup id="cite_ref-288"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-288">[285]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-289"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-289">[286]</a></sup>

Prior to independence, Libya was home to more than 140,000 Christians (mostly of [Italian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italian_settlers_in_Libya "Italian settlers in Libya") and Maltese ancestry). Many Christian settlers left to Italy or Malta after the independence.<sup id="cite_ref-290"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-290">[287]</a></sup> Small foreign communities of Christians remained. [Coptic Orthodox Christianity](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church "Coptic Orthodox Church"), the predominant Christian church of Egypt, is the [largest and most historic Christian denomination in Libya](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_in_Africa#Libya,_Tunisia,_Algeria_and_Morocco "Coptic Orthodox Church in Africa"). There are about 60,000 Egyptian [Copts](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copt "Copt") in Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-291"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-291">[288]</a></sup> There are three Coptic Churches in Libya, one in Tripoli, one in Benghazi, and one in Misurata.

The Coptic Church has grown in recent years in Libya, due to the growing immigration of Egyptian Copts to Libya. There are an estimated 40,000 [Roman Catholics](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Catholic "Roman Catholic") in Libya who are served by two bishops, one in Tripoli (serving the Italian community) and one in [Benghazi](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benghazi "Benghazi") (serving the [Maltese](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maltese_people "Maltese people") community). There is also a small [Anglican](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglican "Anglican") community, made up mostly of African immigrant workers in Tripoli which is part of the [Anglican Diocese of Egypt](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglican_Diocese_of_Egypt "Anglican Diocese of Egypt"). People have been arrested on suspicion of being [Christian missionaries](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_missionaries "Christian missionaries"), as proselytising is illegal.<sup id="cite_ref-292"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-292">[289]</a></sup> Christians have also faced the threat of violence from radical Islamists in some parts of the country, with a well-publicised video released by the Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant in February 2015 depicting the mass beheading of Christian Copts.<sup id="cite_ref-293"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-293">[290]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-294"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-294">[291]</a></sup> Libya was ranked fourth on [Open Doors](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Open_Doors "Open Doors")' 2022 World Watch List, an annual ranking of the 50 countries where Christians face the most extreme persecution.<sup id="cite_ref-295"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-295">[292]</a></sup>

Libya was once the home of one of the oldest Jewish communities in the world, dating back to at least 300 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-296"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-296">[293]</a></sup> In 1942, the Italian Fascist authorities set up forced labor camps south of Tripoli for the Jews, including Giado (about 3,000 Jews), Gharyan, Jeren, and Tigrinna. In Giado some 500 Jews died of weakness, hunger, and disease. In 1942, Jews who were not in the concentration camps were heavily restricted in their economic activity and all men between 18 and 45 years were drafted for forced labor. In August 1942, Jews from Tripolitania were interned in a concentration camp at Sidi Azaz. In the three years after November 1945, more than 140 Jews were murdered, and hundreds more wounded, in a series of [pogroms](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pogrom "Pogrom").<sup id="cite_ref-harris_297-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-harris-297">[294]</a></sup> By 1948, about 38,000 Jews remained in the country. Upon Libyan independence in 1951, most of the Jewish community emigrated.

## Culture [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=28 "Edit section: Culture")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Temple_of_Zeus_-_Cyrene.jpg)

Temple of Zeus in [Cyrene](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyrene,_Libya "Cyrene, Libya")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sabratha_-_Museum_mit_Funden_aus_der_R%C3%B6merzeit,_Mosaik_05.jpg)

Ancient Roman mosaic in [Sabratha](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sabratha "Sabratha")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tripoli_September_1st_Flags.jpg)

[Al-Fatah Revolution day](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1969_Libyan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat "1969 Libyan coup d'état") decorations in [Tripoli](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tripoli,_Libya "Tripoli, Libya") 2008

Many Arabic speaking Libyans consider themselves as part of a wider Arab community. This was strengthened by the spread of Pan-Arabism in the mid-20th century, and their reach to power in Libya where they instituted Arabic as the only official language of the state. Under Gaddafi's rule, the teaching and even use of indigenous [Berber language](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Berber_language "Berber language") was strictly forbidden.<sup id="cite_ref-298"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-298">[295]</a></sup> In addition to banning foreign languages previously taught in academic institutions, leaving entire generations of Libyans with limitations in their comprehension of the English language. Both the spoken Arabic dialects and Berber, still retain words from Italian, that were acquired before and during the _[Libia Italiana](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italian_Libya "Italian Libya")_ period.

Libyans have a heritage in the traditions of the previously nomadic [Bedouin](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bedouin "Bedouin") Arabic speakers and sedentary [Berber](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Berbers "Berbers") tribes. Most Libyans associate themselves with a particular family name originating from tribal or conquest based heritage.

Reflecting the "nature of giving" ([Arabic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_language "Arabic language"): الاحسان __Ihsan__, [Berber languages](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Berber_languages "Berber languages"): ⴰⵏⴰⴽⴽⴰⴼ Anakkaf ), amongst the Libyan people as well as the sense of hospitality, recently the state of Libya made it to the top 20 on the world giving index in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-299"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-299">[296]</a></sup> According to CAF, in a typical month, almost three-quarters (72%) of all Libyans helped somebody they did not know – the third highest level across all 135 countries surveyed.

There are few theaters or art galleries due to the decades of cultural repression under the Qaddafi regime and lack of infrastructure development under the regime of dictatorship.<sup id="cite_ref-300"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-300">[297]</a></sup> For many years there have been no public theaters, and only very few cinemas showing foreign films. The tradition of [folk culture](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Folk_culture "Folk culture") is still alive and well, with troupes performing music and dance at frequent festivals, both in Libya and abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-301"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-301">[298]</a></sup>

A large number of [Libyan television](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Television_in_Libya "Television in Libya") stations are devoted to political review, Islamic topics and cultural phenomena. A number of TV stations air various styles of traditional Libyan music.<sup>[<i>? <a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2012)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> [Tuareg music](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tuareg_music "Tuareg music") and dance are popular in [Ghadames](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghadames "Ghadames") and the south. Libyan television broadcasts air programs mostly in Arabic though usually have time slots for English and French programs.<sup>[<i>? <a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2012)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A 1996 analysis by the [Committee to Protect Journalists](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Committee_to_Protect_Journalists "Committee to Protect Journalists") found Libya's media was the most tightly controlled in the Arab world during the country's dictatorship.<sup id="cite_ref-mediacont_302-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-mediacont-302">[299]</a></sup> As of 2012 hundreds of TV stations have begun to air due to the collapse of censorship from the old regime and the initiation of "free media".

Many Libyans frequent the country's beach and they also visit Libya's archaeological sites—especially [Leptis Magna](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leptis_Magna "Leptis Magna"), which is widely considered to be one of the best preserved Roman archaeological sites in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-303"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-303">[300]</a></sup> The most common form of public transport between cities is the bus, though many people travel by automobile. There are no railway services in Libya, but these are planned for construction in the near future (see [rail transport in Libya](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rail_transport_in_Libya "Rail transport in Libya")).<sup id="cite_ref-304"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-304">[301]</a></sup>

Libya's capital, [Tripoli](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tripoli,_Libya "Tripoli, Libya"), has many museums and archives. These include the Government Library, the Ethnographic Museum, the Archaeological Museum, the National Archives, the Epigraphy Museum and the Islamic Museum. The [Red Castle Museum](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_Castle_Museum "Red Castle Museum") located in the capital near the coast and right in the city center, built in consultation with [UNESCO](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/UNESCO "UNESCO"), may be the country's most famous.<sup id="cite_ref-305"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-305">[302]</a></sup>

### Cuisine [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=29 "Edit section: Cuisine")

 [](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bazin.jpg)

[Bazeen](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bazin_(bread) "Bazin (bread)"), a communal bread dish

Libyan cuisine is a mixture of the different [Italian](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italian_cuisine "Italian cuisine"), Bedouin and traditional Arab culinary influences.<sup id="cite_ref-306"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-306">[303]</a></sup> Pasta is the staple food in the Western side of Libya, whereas rice is generally the staple food in the east.

Common Libyan foods include several variations of red (tomato) sauce based pasta dishes (similar to the Italian [Sugo all'arrabbiata](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arrabbiata_sauce "Arrabbiata sauce") dish); rice, usually served with lamb or chicken (typically stewed, fried, grilled, or boiled in-sauce); and [couscous](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Couscous "Couscous"), which is steam cooked whilst held over boiling red (tomato) sauce and meat (sometimes also containing courgettes/zucchini and chickpeas), which is typically served along with cucumber slices, lettuce and olives.

[Bazeen](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bazeen "Bazeen"), a dish made from barley flour and served with red tomato sauce, is customarily eaten communally, with several people sharing the same dish, usually by hand. This dish is commonly served at traditional weddings or festivities. [Asida](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asida "Asida") is a sweet version of Bazeen, made from white flour and served with a mix of honey, ghee or butter. Another favorite way to serve Asida is with [rub](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rub_(syrup) "Rub (syrup)") (fresh date syrup) and olive oil. [Usban](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Usban "Usban") is animal tripe stitched and stuffed with rice and vegetables cooked in tomato based soup or steamed. [Shurba](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chorba "Chorba") is a red tomato sauce-based soup, usually served with small grains of pasta.<sup id="cite_ref-307"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-307">[304]</a></sup>

A very common snack eaten by Libyans is known as _khubs bi' tun_, literally meaning "bread with tuna fish", usually served as a baked baguette or pita bread stuffed with tuna fish that has been mixed with [harissa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harissa "Harissa") (chili sauce) and olive oil. Many snack vendors prepare these sandwiches and they can be found all over Libya. Libyan restaurants may serve international cuisine, or may serve simpler fare such as lamb, chicken, vegetable stew, potatoes and [macaroni](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macaroni "Macaroni").<sup>[<i><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Due to severe lack of infrastructure, many under-developed areas and small towns do not have restaurants and instead food stores may be the only source to obtain food products. Alcohol consumption is illegal in the entire country.<sup id="cite_ref-308"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-308">[305]</a></sup>

There are four main ingredients of traditional Libyan food: [olives](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olive "Olive") (and [olive oil](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olive_oil "Olive oil")), [dates](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Date_palm "Date palm"), grains and milk.<sup id="cite_ref-Libyan_Food_309-0"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Libyan_Food-309">[306]</a></sup> Grains are roasted, ground, sieved and used for making bread, cakes, soups and bazeen. Dates are harvested, dried and can be eaten as they are, made into syrup or slightly fried and eaten with [bsisa](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bsisa "Bsisa") and milk. After eating, Libyans often drink black tea. This is normally repeated a second time (for the second glass of tea), and in the third round of tea, it is served with roasted peanuts or roasted [almonds](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Almond "Almond") known as _shay bi'l-luz_ (mixed with the tea in the same glass).<sup id="cite_ref-Libyan_Food_309-1"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-Libyan_Food-309">[306]</a></sup>

### Sport [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=30 "Edit section: Sport")

[Football](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Association_football "Association football") is the most popular sport in Libya. The country hosted the [1982 African Cup of Nations](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1982_African_Cup_of_Nations "1982 African Cup of Nations") and almost qualified for the [1986 FIFA World Cup](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/1986_FIFA_World_Cup "1986 FIFA World Cup"). The [national team](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya_national_football_team "Libya national football team") almost won the 1982 AFCON; they lost to [Ghana](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ghana_national_football_team "Ghana national football team") on penalties 7–6. In 2014, Libya won the African Nations Championship after beating Ghana in the finals. Although the national team has never won a major competition or qualified for a World Cup, there is still lots of passion for the sport and the quality of football is improving.<sup id="cite_ref-310"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-310">[307]</a></sup> It also participated in many Summer Olympics, such as the [2016 Summer Olympics](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/2016_Summer_Olympics "2016 Summer Olympics"), the [2008 Summer Olympics](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympics "2008 Summer Olympics") and more.

Horse racing is also a popular sport in Libya. It is a tradition of many special occasions and holidays.<sup id="cite_ref-311"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-311">[308]</a></sup>

## Notes [edit](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Libya&action=edit&section=32 "Edit section: Notes")

1.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_ref-5 "Jump up")** Disputed by [Osama Hamada](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Osama_Hamada "Osama Hamada")<sup id="cite_ref-4"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> of the [Government of National Stability](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_National_Stability "Government of National Stability")

1.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_ref-9 "Jump up")** [_LIB\-ee-ə_](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key"); [Arabic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_language "Arabic language"): ليبيا, <small><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romanization_of_Arabic" title="Romanization of Arabic">romanized</a>:&nbsp;</small> _Lībiyā_, pronounced [\[liː.bi.jæː\]](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic "Help:IPA/Arabic")
2.  **[^](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_ref-14 "Jump up")** [Arabic](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_language "Arabic language"): دولة ليبيا, <small><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romanization_of_Arabic" title="Romanization of Arabic">romanized</a>:&nbsp;</small> _Dawlat Lībiyā_ <sup id="cite_ref-10"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-10">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-11">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13"><a href="https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libya#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup>
